When the tRNA binds to its complementary codon in an mRNA strand, the ribosome attaches its amino acid cargo to the new polypeptide chain, which is synthesized from <a href="/wiki/N-terminus" title="N-terminus">amino terminus</a> to <a href="/wiki/C-terminus" title="C-terminus">carboxyl terminus</a>. During and after synthesis, most new proteins must <a href="/wiki/Protein_folding" title="Protein folding">fold</a> to their active <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tertiary_structure" title="Tertiary structure">three-dimensional structure</a> before they can carry out their cellular functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MBOC_46-19"><a href="#cite_note-MBOC-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 3">: 3 </span></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulation">Regulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Regulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_gene_expression" title="Regulation of gene expression">Genes are regulated</a> so that they are <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">expressed</a> only when the product is needed, since expression draws on limited resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MBOC_46-20"><a href="#cite_note-MBOC-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 7">: 7 </span></sup> A cell regulates its gene expression depending on its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environment_(biophysical)" title="Environment (biophysical)">external environment</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">available nutrients</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heat_shock_protein" title="Heat shock protein">temperature</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Cellular_stress_response" title="Cellular stress response">stresses</a>), its internal environment (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cell_division_cycle" title="Cell division cycle">cell division cycle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infection status</a>), and its <a href="/wiki/Cellular_differentiation" title="Cellular differentiation">specific role</a> if in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multicellular" title="Multicellular">multicellular</a> organism. Gene expression can be regulated at any step: from <a href="/wiki/Transcriptional_regulation" title="Transcriptional regulation">transcriptional initiation</a>, to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RNA_processing" title="RNA processing">RNA processing</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Post-translational_modification" title="Post-translational modification">post-translational modification</a> of the protein. The regulation of <a href="/wiki/Lactose" title="Lactose">lactose</a> metabolism genes in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E._coli" title="E. coli">E. coli</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Lac_operon" title="Lac operon"><i>lac</i> operon</a>) was the first such mechanism to be described in 1961.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="RNA_genes">RNA genes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: RNA genes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A typical protein-coding gene is first copied into <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> as an intermediate in the manufacture of the final protein product.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MBOC_46-21"><a href="#cite_note-MBOC-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 6.1">: 6.1 </span></sup> In other cases, the RNA molecules are the actual functional products, as in the synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Ribosomal_RNA" title="Ribosomal RNA">ribosomal RNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transfer_RNA" title="Transfer RNA">transfer RNA</a>. Some RNAs known as <a href="/wiki/Ribozyme" title="Ribozyme">ribozymes</a> are capable of <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymatic function</a>, and <a href="/wiki/MicroRNA" title="MicroRNA">microRNA</a> has a regulatory role. The <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> sequences from which such RNAs are transcribed are known as <a href="/wiki/Non-coding_RNA" title="Non-coding RNA">non-coding RNA genes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edd01_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Edd01-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a> store their entire genomes in the form of <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>, and contain no DNA at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Because they use RNA to store genes, their <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cellular</a> <a href="/wiki/Host_(biology)" title="Host (biology)">hosts</a> may synthesize their proteins as soon as they are <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infected</a> and without the delay in waiting for transcription.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> On the other hand, RNA <a href="/wiki/Retrovirus" title="Retrovirus">retroviruses</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a>, require the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reverse_transcription" title="Reverse transcription">reverse transcription</a> of their <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> from RNA into DNA before their proteins can be synthesized. RNA-mediated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epigenetic" title="Epigenetic">epigenetic</a> inheritance has also been observed in plants and very rarely in animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morris_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-morris-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Inheritance">Inheritance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Inheritance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Autosomal_recessive_-_mini.svg"><img alt="Illustration of autosomal recessive inheritance. Each parent has one blue allele and one white allele. Each of their 4 children inherit one allele from each parent such that one child ends up with two blue alleles, one child has two white alleles and two children have one of each allele. Only the child with both blue alleles shows the trait because the trait is recessive." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1561" data-file-width="1201" decoding="async" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Autosomal_recessive_-_mini.svg/220px-Autosomal_recessive_-_mini.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Autosomal_recessive_-_mini.svg/330px-Autosomal_recessive_-_mini.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Autosomal_recessive_-_mini.svg/440px-Autosomal_recessive_-_mini.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Autosomal_recessive_-_mini.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Inheritance of a gene that has two different <a href="/wiki/Allele" title="Allele">alleles</a> (blue and white). The gene is located on an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autosomal_chromosome" title="Autosomal chromosome">autosomal chromosome</a>. The white allele is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recessive" title="Recessive">recessive</a> to the blue allele. The probability of each outcome in the children's generation is one quarter, or 25 percent.</div></div></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Mendelian_inheritance" title="Mendelian inheritance">Mendelian inheritance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heredity" title="Heredity">Heredity</a></div><p>Organisms inherit their genes from their parents. <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction">Asexual</a> organisms simply inherit a complete copy of their parent's genome. <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">Sexual</a> organisms have two copies of each chromosome because they inherit one complete set from each parent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MBOC_46-22"><a href="#cite_note-MBOC-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1">: 1 </span></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mendelian_inheritance">Mendelian inheritance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Mendelian inheritance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Mendelian_inheritance" title="Mendelian inheritance">Mendelian inheritance</a>, variations in an organism's <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotype</a> (observable physical and behavioral characteristics) are due in part to variations in its <a href="/wiki/Genotype" title="Genotype">genotype</a> (particular set of genes). Each gene specifies a particular trait with a different sequence of a gene (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alleles" title="Alleles">alleles</a>) giving rise to different phenotypes. Most eukaryotic organisms (such as the pea plants Mendel worked on) have two alleles for each trait, one inherited from each parent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MBOC_46-23"><a href="#cite_note-MBOC-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 20">: 20 </span></sup>
</p><p>Alleles at a locus may be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dominant_gene" title="Dominant gene">dominant</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recessive_gene" title="Recessive gene">recessive</a>; dominant alleles give rise to their corresponding phenotypes when paired with any other allele for the same trait, whereas recessive alleles give rise to their corresponding phenotype only when paired with another copy of the same allele. If you know the genotypes of the organisms, you can determine which alleles are dominant and which are recessive. For example, if the allele specifying tall stems in pea plants is dominant over the allele specifying short stems, then pea plants that inherit one tall allele from one parent and one short allele from the other parent will also have tall stems. Mendel's work demonstrated that alleles assort independently in the production of <a href="/wiki/Gamete" title="Gamete">gametes</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Germ_cell" title="Germ cell">germ cells</a>, ensuring variation in the next generation. Although Mendelian inheritance remains a good model for many traits determined by single genes (including a number of well-known <a href="/wiki/Genetic_disorder#Single-gene_disorder" title="Genetic disorder">genetic disorders</a>) it does not include the physical processes of DNA replication and cell division.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scitable_miko_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-scitable_miko-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scitable_chial_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-scitable_chial-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="DNA_replication_and_cell_division">DNA replication and cell division</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: DNA replication and cell division">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The growth, development, and reproduction of organisms relies on <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">cell division</a>; the process by which a single <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cell</a> divides into two usually identical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daughter_cell" title="Daughter cell">daughter cells</a>. This requires first making a duplicate copy of every gene in the <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> in a process called <a href="/wiki/DNA_replication" title="DNA replication">DNA replication</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MBOC_46-24"><a href="#cite_note-MBOC-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 5.2">: 5.2 </span></sup> The copies are made by specialized <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> known as <a href="/wiki/DNA_polymerase" title="DNA polymerase">DNA polymerases</a>, which "reads" one strand of the double-helical DNA, known as the template strand, and synthesize a new complementary strand. Because the DNA double helix is held together by <a href="/wiki/Base_pair" title="Base pair">base pairing</a>, the sequence of one strand completely specifies the sequence of its complement; hence only one strand needs to be read by the enzyme to produce a faithful copy. The process of DNA replication is <a href="/wiki/Semiconservative_replication" title="Semiconservative replication">semiconservative</a>; that is, the copy of the genome inherited by each daughter cell contains one original and one newly synthesized strand of DNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MBOC_46-25"><a href="#cite_note-MBOC-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 5.2">: 5.2 </span></sup>
</p><p>The rate of DNA replication in living cells was first measured as the rate of phage T4 DNA elongation in phage-infected <i>E. coli</i> and found to be impressively rapid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid789903_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid789903-81">[81]</a></sup> During the period of exponential DNA increase at 37 °C, the rate of elongation was 749 nucleotides per second.
</p><p>After DNA replication is complete, the cell must physically separate the two copies of the genome and divide into two distinct membrane-bound cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MBOC_46-26"><a href="#cite_note-MBOC-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 18.2">: 18.2 </span></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotes</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a>) this usually occurs via a relatively simple process called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_fission" title="Binary fission">binary fission</a>, in which each circular genome attaches to the <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membrane</a> and is separated into the daughter cells as the membrane <a href="/wiki/Invagination" title="Invagination">invaginates</a> to split the <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasm</a> into two membrane-bound portions. Binary fission is extremely fast compared to the rates of cell division in <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a>. Eukaryotic cell division is a more complex process known as the <a href="/wiki/Cell_cycle" title="Cell cycle">cell cycle</a>; DNA replication occurs during a phase of this cycle known as <a href="/wiki/S_phase" title="S phase">S phase</a>, whereas the process of segregating <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosomes</a> and splitting the <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasm</a> occurs during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M_phase" title="M phase">M phase</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MBOC_46-27"><a href="#cite_note-MBOC-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 18.1">: 18.1 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Molecular_inheritance">Molecular inheritance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Molecular inheritance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The duplication and transmission of genetic material from one generation of cells to the next is the basis for molecular inheritance and the link between the classical and molecular pictures of genes. Organisms inherit the characteristics of their parents because the cells of the offspring contain copies of the genes in their parents' cells. In <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction">asexually reproducing</a> organisms, the offspring will be a genetic copy or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clone_(genetics)" title="Clone (genetics)">clone</a> of the parent organism. In <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">sexually reproducing</a> organisms, a specialized form of cell division called <a href="/wiki/Meiosis" title="Meiosis">meiosis</a> produces cells called <a href="/wiki/Gamete" title="Gamete">gametes</a> or <a href="/wiki/Germ_cell" title="Germ cell">germ cells</a> that are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haploid" title="Haploid">haploid</a>, or contain only one copy of each gene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MBOC_46-28"><a href="#cite_note-MBOC-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 20.2">: 20.2 </span></sup> The gametes produced by females are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_(biology)" title="Egg (biology)">eggs</a> or ova, and those produced by males are called <a href="/wiki/Sperm" title="Sperm">sperm</a>. Two gametes fuse to form a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diploid" title="Diploid">diploid</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertilized_egg" title="Fertilized egg">fertilized egg</a>, a single cell that has two sets of genes, with one copy of each gene from the mother and one from the father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MBOC_46-29"><a href="#cite_note-MBOC-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 20">: 20 </span></sup>
</p><p>During the process of meiotic cell division, an event called <a href="/wiki/Genetic_recombination" title="Genetic recombination">genetic recombination</a> or <i>crossing-over</i> can sometimes occur, in which a length of DNA on one <a href="/wiki/Chromatid" title="Chromatid">chromatid</a> is swapped with a length of DNA on the corresponding homologous non-sister chromatid. This can result in reassortment of otherwise linked alleles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MBOC_46-30"><a href="#cite_note-MBOC-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 5.5">: 5.5 </span></sup> The Mendelian principle of independent assortment asserts that each of a parent's two genes for each trait will sort independently into gametes; which allele an organism inherits for one trait is unrelated to which allele it inherits for another trait. This is in fact only true for genes that do not reside on the same chromosome or are located very far from one another on the same chromosome. The closer two genes lie on the same chromosome, the more closely they will be associated in gametes and the more often they will appear together (known as <a href="/wiki/Genetic_linkage" title="Genetic linkage">genetic linkage</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scitable_lobo_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-scitable_lobo-82">[82]</a></sup> Genes that are very close are essentially never separated because it is extremely unlikely that a crossover point will occur between them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scitable_lobo_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-scitable_lobo-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Molecular_evolution">Molecular evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Molecular evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Molecular_evolution" title="Molecular evolution">Molecular evolution</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mutation">Mutation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Mutation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>DNA replication is for the most part extremely accurate, however errors (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutations" title="Mutations">mutations</a>) do occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MBOC_46-31"><a href="#cite_note-MBOC-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 7.6">: 7.6 </span></sup> The error rate in <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotic</a> <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a> can be as low as 10<sup>−8</sup> per <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotide</a> per replication,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nachman_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nachman-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science2_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Science2-84">[84]</a></sup> whereas for some RNA viruses it can be as high as 10<sup>−3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genetics2_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics2-85">[85]</a></sup> This means that each generation, each human genome accumulates 1–2 new mutations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genetics2_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics2-85">[85]</a></sup> Small mutations can be caused by <a href="/wiki/DNA_replication" title="DNA replication">DNA replication</a> and the aftermath of <a href="/wiki/DNA_repair" title="DNA repair">DNA damage</a> and include <a href="/wiki/Point_mutation" title="Point mutation">point mutations</a> in which a single base is altered and <a href="/wiki/Frameshift_mutation" title="Frameshift mutation">frameshift mutations</a> in which a single base is inserted or deleted. Either of these mutations can change the gene by <a href="/wiki/Missense_mutation" title="Missense mutation">missense</a> (change a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Codon" title="Codon">codon</a> to encode a different amino acid) or <a href="/wiki/Nonsense_mutation" title="Nonsense mutation">nonsense</a> (a premature <a href="/wiki/Stop_codon" title="Stop codon">stop codon</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Larger mutations can be caused by errors in recombination to cause <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chromosomal_abnormality" title="Chromosomal abnormality">chromosomal abnormalities</a> including the <a href="/wiki/Gene_duplication" title="Gene duplication">duplication</a>, deletion, rearrangement or inversion of large sections of a chromosome. Additionally, DNA repair mechanisms can introduce mutational errors when repairing physical damage to the molecule. The repair, even with mutation, is more important to survival than restoring an exact copy, for example when repairing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double-strand_breaks" title="Double-strand breaks">double-strand breaks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MBOC_46-32"><a href="#cite_note-MBOC-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 5.4">: 5.4 </span></sup>
</p><p>When multiple different <a href="/wiki/Allele" title="Allele">alleles</a> for a gene are present in a species's population it is called <a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(biology)" title="Polymorphism (biology)">polymorphic</a>. Most different alleles are functionally equivalent, however some alleles can give rise to different <a href="/wiki/Phenotypic_trait" title="Phenotypic trait">phenotypic traits</a>. A gene's most common allele is called the <a href="/wiki/Wild_type" title="Wild type">wild type</a>, and rare alleles are called <a href="/wiki/Mutant" title="Mutant">mutants</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Genetic_variation" title="Genetic variation">genetic variation</a> in relative frequencies of different alleles in a population is due to both <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genetic_drift" title="Genetic drift">genetic drift</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> The wild-type allele is not necessarily the <a href="/wiki/Ancestor" title="Ancestor">ancestor</a> of less common alleles, nor is it necessarily <a href="/wiki/Fitness_(biology)" title="Fitness (biology)">fitter</a>.
</p><p>Most mutations within genes are <a href="/wiki/Neutral_mutation" title="Neutral mutation">neutral</a>, having no effect on the organism's phenotype (<a href="/wiki/Silent_mutation" title="Silent mutation">silent mutations</a>). Some mutations do not change the amino acid sequence because multiple codons encode the same amino acid (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synonymous_mutations" title="Synonymous mutations">synonymous mutations</a>). Other mutations can be neutral if they lead to amino acid sequence changes, but the protein still functions similarly with the new amino acid (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservative_mutation" title="Conservative mutation">conservative mutations</a>). Many mutations, however, are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deleterious_mutation" title="Deleterious mutation">deleterious</a> or even <a href="/wiki/Lethal_allele" title="Lethal allele">lethal</a>, and are removed from populations by natural selection. Genetic disorders are the result of deleterious mutations and can be due to spontaneous mutation in the affected individual, or can be inherited. Finally, a small fraction of mutations are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beneficial_mutation" title="Beneficial mutation">beneficial</a>, improving the organism's <a href="/wiki/Fitness_(biology)" title="Fitness (biology)">fitness</a> and are extremely important for evolution, since their <a href="/wiki/Directional_selection" title="Directional selection">directional selection</a> leads to adaptive <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MBOC_46-33"><a href="#cite_note-MBOC-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 7.6">: 7.6 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sequence_homology">Sequence homology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Sequence homology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Histone_Alignment.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="410" data-file-width="1190" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Histone_Alignment.png/400px-Histone_Alignment.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Histone_Alignment.png/600px-Histone_Alignment.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Histone_Alignment.png/800px-Histone_Alignment.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Histone_Alignment.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sequence alignment, produced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ClustalO" title="ClustalO">ClustalO</a>, of mammalian <a href="/wiki/Histone" title="Histone">histone</a> proteins</div></div></div>
<p>Genes with a <a href="/wiki/Most_recent_common_ancestor" title="Most recent common ancestor">most recent common ancestor</a>, and thus a shared evolutionary ancestry, are known as <a href="/wiki/Sequence_homology" title="Sequence homology">homologs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> These genes appear either from gene duplication within an organism's genome, where they are known as paralogous genes, or are the result of divergence of the genes after a <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">speciation</a> event, where they are known as orthologous genes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MBOC_46-34"><a href="#cite_note-MBOC-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 7.6">: 7.6 </span></sup> and often perform the same or similar functions in related organisms. It is often assumed that the functions of orthologous genes are more similar than those of paralogous genes, although the difference is minimal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>The relationship between genes can be measured by comparing the <a href="/wiki/Sequence_alignment" title="Sequence alignment">sequence alignment</a> of their DNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MBOC_46-35"><a href="#cite_note-MBOC-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 7.6">: 7.6 </span></sup> The degree of sequence similarity between homologous genes is called <a href="/wiki/Conserved_sequence" title="Conserved sequence">conserved sequence</a>. Most changes to a gene's sequence do not affect its function and so genes accumulate mutations over time by <a href="/wiki/Neutral_theory_of_molecular_evolution" title="Neutral theory of molecular evolution">neutral molecular evolution</a>. Additionally, any selection on a gene will cause its sequence to diverge at a different rate. Genes under <a href="/wiki/Stabilizing_selection" title="Stabilizing selection">stabilizing selection</a> are <a href="/wiki/Biological_constraints" title="Biological constraints">constrained</a> and so change more slowly whereas genes under <a href="/wiki/Directional_selection" title="Directional selection">directional selection</a> change sequence more rapidly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The sequence differences between genes can be used for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogenetic" title="Phylogenetic">phylogenetic</a> analyses to study how those genes have evolved and how the organisms they come from are related.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scitable_mitchell_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-scitable_mitchell-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_of_new_genes">Origins of new genes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Origins of new genes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Evolution_fate_duplicate_genes_-_vector.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="576" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Evolution_fate_duplicate_genes_-_vector.svg/400px-Evolution_fate_duplicate_genes_-_vector.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Evolution_fate_duplicate_genes_-_vector.svg/600px-Evolution_fate_duplicate_genes_-_vector.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Evolution_fate_duplicate_genes_-_vector.svg/800px-Evolution_fate_duplicate_genes_-_vector.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Evolution_fate_duplicate_genes_-_vector.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Evolutionary fate of duplicate genes.</div></div></div>
<p>The most common source of new genes in eukaryotic lineages is <a href="/wiki/Gene_duplication" title="Gene duplication">gene duplication</a>, which creates <a href="/wiki/Copy_number_variation" title="Copy number variation">copy number variation</a> of an existing gene in the genome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guerzoni_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-guerzoni-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The resulting genes (paralogs) may then diverge in sequence and in function. Sets of genes formed in this way compose a <a href="/wiki/Gene_family" title="Gene family">gene family</a>. Gene duplications and losses within a family are common and represent a major source of evolutionary <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Sometimes, gene duplication may result in a nonfunctional copy of a gene, or a functional copy may be subject to mutations that result in loss of function; such nonfunctional genes are called <a href="/wiki/Pseudogene" title="Pseudogene">pseudogenes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MBOC_46-36"><a href="#cite_note-MBOC-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 7.6">: 7.6 </span></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Orphan_gene" title="Orphan gene">"Orphan" genes</a>, whose sequence shows no similarity to existing genes, are less common than gene duplicates. The human genome contains an estimate 18<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> to 60<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> genes with no identifiable homologs outside humans. Orphan genes arise primarily from either <a href="/wiki/De_novo_gene_birth" title="De novo gene birth"><i>de novo</i> emergence</a> from previously <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-coding_sequence" title="Non-coding sequence">non-coding sequence</a>, or gene duplication followed by such rapid sequence change that the original relationship becomes undetectable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> <i>De novo</i> genes are typically shorter and simpler in structure than most eukaryotic genes, with few if any introns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guerzoni_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-guerzoni-94">[94]</a></sup> Over long evolutionary time periods, <i>de novo</i> gene birth may be responsible for a significant fraction of taxonomically restricted gene families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer" title="Horizontal gene transfer">Horizontal gene transfer</a> refers to the transfer of genetic material through a mechanism other than <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">reproduction</a>. This mechanism is a common source of new genes in <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotes</a>, sometimes thought to contribute more to genetic variation than gene duplication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> It is a common means of spreading <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance" title="Antibiotic resistance">antibiotic resistance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virulence" title="Virulence">virulence</a>, and adaptive <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolic</a> functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bennett_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-bennett-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Although horizontal gene transfer is rare in eukaryotes, likely examples have been identified of <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protist</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alga" title="Alga">alga</a> genomes containing genes of bacterial origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genome">Genome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Genome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> is the total genetic material of an organism and includes both the genes and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-coding_sequence" title="Non-coding sequence">non-coding sequences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Eukaryotic genes can be annotated using FINDER.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid33879057_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid33879057-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Number_of_genes">Number of genes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Number of genes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:612px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gene_numbers.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="376" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Gene_numbers.svg/610px-Gene_numbers.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Gene_numbers.svg/915px-Gene_numbers.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Gene_numbers.svg/1220px-Gene_numbers.svg.png 2x" width="610"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gene_numbers.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Depiction of numbers of genes for representative <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a> (green), <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> (blue), <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> (orange), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a> (yellow), <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> (purple), and <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a> (grey). An inset on the right shows the smaller genomes expanded 100-fold area-wise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WatsonJD_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-WatsonJD-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11935017_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11935017-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mitochondrial_genome_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-mitochondrial_genome-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-114">[114]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Genome_size" title="Genome size">genome size</a>, and the number of genes it encodes varies widely between organisms. The smallest genomes occur in <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Viroid" title="Viroid">viroids</a> (which act as a single non-coding RNA gene).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Conversely, plants can have extremely large genomes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> with <a href="/wiki/Oryza_sativa" title="Oryza sativa">rice</a> containing &gt;46,000 protein-coding genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11935017_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11935017-111">[111]</a></sup> The total number of protein-coding genes (the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Proteome" title="Proteome">proteome</a>) is estimated to be 5 million sequences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the number of base-pairs of DNA in the human genome has been known since the 1960s, the estimated number of genes has changed over time as definitions of genes, and methods of detecting them have been refined. Initial theoretical predictions of the number of human genes were as high as 2,000,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Early experimental measures indicated there to be 50,000–100,000 <i>transcribed</i> genes (<a href="/wiki/Expressed_sequence_tag" title="Expressed sequence tag">expressed sequence tags</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Subsequently, the sequencing in the <a href="/wiki/Human_Genome_Project" title="Human Genome Project">Human Genome Project</a> indicated that many of these transcripts were <a href="/wiki/Alternative_splicing" title="Alternative splicing">alternative variants</a> of the same genes, and the total number of protein-coding genes was revised down to ~20,000<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-114">[114]</a></sup> with 13 genes encoded on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">mitochondrial</a> genome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mitochondrial_genome_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-mitochondrial_genome-112">[112]</a></sup> With the <a href="/wiki/GENCODE" title="GENCODE">GENCODE</a> annotation project, that estimate has continued to fall to 19,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Of the human genome, only 1–2% consists of protein-coding sequences,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Claverie2005_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Claverie2005-122">[122]</a></sup> with the remainder being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noncoding_DNA" title="Noncoding DNA">'noncoding' DNA</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Intron" title="Intron">introns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Retrotransposon" title="Retrotransposon">retrotransposons</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noncoding_RNA" title="Noncoding RNA">noncoding RNAs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Claverie2005_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Claverie2005-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carninci2007_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carninci2007-123">[123]</a></sup> Every multicellular organism has all its genes in each cell of its body but not every gene functions in every cell .
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Essential_genes">Essential genes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Essential genes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Essential_gene" title="Essential gene">Essential gene</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Syn3_genome.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="203" data-file-width="340" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Syn3_genome.svg/280px-Syn3_genome.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Syn3_genome.svg/420px-Syn3_genome.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Syn3_genome.svg/560px-Syn3_genome.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Syn3_genome.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gene functions in the minimal <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_biology" title="Synthetic biology">synthetic organism</a>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syn_3" title="Syn 3">Syn 3</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchison_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hutchison-124">[124]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Essential genes are the set of genes thought to be critical for an organism's survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> This definition assumes the abundant availability of all relevant <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrients</a> and the absence of environmental stress. Only a small portion of an organism's genes are essential. In bacteria, an estimated 250–400 genes are essential for <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">Escherichia coli</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_subtilis" title="Bacillus subtilis">Bacillus subtilis</a></i>, which is less than 10% of their genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-128">[128]</a></sup> Half of these genes are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ortholog" title="Ortholog">orthologs</a> in both organisms and are largely involved in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protein_synthesis" title="Protein synthesis">protein synthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-128">[128]</a></sup> In the budding yeast <i><a href="/wiki/Saccharomyces_cerevisiae" title="Saccharomyces cerevisiae">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</a></i> the number of essential genes is slightly higher, at 1000 genes (~20% of their genes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-129">[129]</a></sup> Although the number is more difficult to measure in higher eukaryotes, mice and humans are estimated to have around 2000 essential genes (~10% of their genes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-georgi_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-georgi-130">[130]</a></sup> The synthetic organism, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syn_3" title="Syn 3">Syn 3</a></i>, has a minimal genome of 473 essential genes and quasi-essential genes (necessary for fast growth), although 149 have unknown function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchison_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hutchison-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>Essential genes include <a href="/wiki/Housekeeping_gene" title="Housekeeping gene">housekeeping genes</a> (critical for basic cell functions)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> as well as genes that are expressed at different times in the organisms <a href="/wiki/Developmental_biology" title="Developmental biology">development</a> or <a href="/wiki/Biological_life_cycle" title="Biological life cycle">life cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Housekeeping genes are used as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Experimental_control" title="Experimental control">experimental controls</a> when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gene_expression_analysis" title="Gene expression analysis">analysing gene expression</a>, since they are <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">constitutively expressed</a> at a relatively constant level.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_and_genomic_nomenclature">Genetic and genomic nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Genetic and genomic nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gene_nomenclature" title="Gene nomenclature">Gene nomenclature</a> has been established by the <a href="/wiki/HUGO_Gene_Nomenclature_Committee" title="HUGO Gene Nomenclature Committee">HUGO Gene Nomenclature Committee</a> (HGNC), a committee of the <a href="/wiki/Human_Genome_Organisation" title="Human Genome Organisation">Human Genome Organisation</a>, for each known human gene in the form of an approved gene name and <a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">symbol</a> (short-form <a href="/wiki/Abbreviation" title="Abbreviation">abbreviation</a>), which can be accessed through a database maintained by HGNC. Symbols are chosen to be unique, and each gene has only one symbol (although approved symbols sometimes change). Symbols are preferably kept consistent with other members of a <a href="/wiki/Gene_family" title="Gene family">gene family</a> and with homologs in other species, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Mouse" title="Mouse">mouse</a> due to its role as a common <a href="/wiki/Model_organism" title="Model organism">model organism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_engineering">Genetic engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Genetic engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Breeding_transgenesis_cisgenesis.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="921" data-file-width="1077" decoding="async" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Breeding_transgenesis_cisgenesis.svg/300px-Breeding_transgenesis_cisgenesis.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Breeding_transgenesis_cisgenesis.svg/450px-Breeding_transgenesis_cisgenesis.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Breeding_transgenesis_cisgenesis.svg/600px-Breeding_transgenesis_cisgenesis.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Breeding_transgenesis_cisgenesis.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of conventional plant breeding with transgenic and cisgenic genetic modification.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">Genetic engineering</a></div>
<p>Genetic engineering is the modification of an organism's <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> through <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a>. Since the 1970s, a <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering_techniques" title="Genetic engineering techniques">variety of techniques</a> have been developed to specifically add, remove and edit genes in an organism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Recently developed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genome_engineering" title="Genome engineering">genome engineering</a> techniques use engineered <a href="/wiki/Nuclease" title="Nuclease">nuclease</a> <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> to create targeted <a href="/wiki/DNA_repair" title="DNA repair">DNA repair</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosome</a> to either disrupt or edit a gene when the break is repaired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ran_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-ran-138">[138]</a></sup> The related term <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_biology" title="Synthetic biology">synthetic biology</a> is sometimes used to refer to extensive genetic engineering of an organism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>Genetic engineering is now a routine research tool with <a href="/wiki/Model_organism" title="Model organism">model organisms</a>. For example, genes are easily added to <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> and lineages of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Knockout_mice" title="Knockout mice">knockout mice</a> with a specific gene's function disrupted are used to investigate that gene's function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Many organisms have been genetically modified for applications in <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, industrial biotechnology, and <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>.
</p><p>For multicellular organisms, typically the <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryo</a> is engineered which grows into the adult <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">genetically modified organism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deng_2007_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deng_2007-143">[143]</a></sup> However, the genomes of cells in an adult organism can be edited using <a href="/wiki/Gene_therapy" title="Gene therapy">gene therapy</a> techniques to treat genetic diseases.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Rethink-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Rethink_10-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Rethink_10-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Rethink_10-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFPennisi2007"><a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Pennisi" title="Elizabeth Pennisi">Pennisi E</a> (June 2007). "Genomics. DNA study forces rethink of what it means to be a gene". <i>Science</i>. <b>316</b> (5831): 1556–7. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1126%2Fscience.316.5831.1556" rel="nofollow">10.1126/science.316.5831.1556</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/17569836" rel="nofollow">17569836</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a> <a class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:36463252" rel="nofollow">36463252</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Science&amp;rft.atitle=Genomics.+DNA+study+forces+rethink+of+what+it+means+to+be+a+gene&amp;rft.volume=316&amp;rft.issue=5831&amp;rft.pages=1556-7&amp;rft.date=2007-06&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A36463252%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F17569836&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1126%2Fscience.316.5831.1556&amp;rft.aulast=Pennisi&amp;rft.aufirst=E&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGene"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Johannsen-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Johannsen_11-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Johannsen_11-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1 cs1-prop-foreign-lang-source" id="CITEREFJohannsen1909">Johannsen W (1909). <a class="external text" href="https://www.biodiversitylibrary.org/item/15717#page/134/mode/1up" rel="nofollow"><i>Elemente der exakten Erblichkeitslehre</i></a> [<i>Elements of the exact theory of heredity</i>] (in German). Jena, Germany: Gustav Fischer. p. 124.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Elemente+der+exakten+Erblichkeitslehre&amp;rft.place=Jena%2C+Germany&amp;rft.pages=124&amp;rft.pub=Gustav+Fischer&amp;rft.date=1909&amp;rft.aulast=Johannsen&amp;rft.aufirst=W&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.biodiversitylibrary.org%2Fitem%2F15717%23page%2F134%2Fmode%2F1up&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGene"></span>  From p. 124:  <i>"Dieses "etwas" in den Gameten bezw. in der Zygote, … – kurz, was wir eben Gene nennen wollen – bedingt sind."</i>  (This "something" in the gametes or in the zygote, which has crucial importance for the character of the organism, is usually called by the quite ambiguous term <i>Anlagen</i> [primordium, from the German word <i>Anlage</i> for "plan, arrangement ; rough sketch"].  Many other terms have been suggested, mostly unfortunately in closer connection with certain hypothetical opinions.  The word "pangene", which was introduced by Darwin, is perhaps used most frequently in place of <i>Anlagen</i>.  However, the word "pangene" was not well chosen, as it is a compound word containing the roots <i>pan</i> (the neuter form of Πας all, every) and <i>gen</i> (from γί-γ(ε)ν-ομαι, to become).  Only the meaning of this latter [i.e., <i>gen</i>] comes into consideration here ; just the basic idea – [namely,] that a trait in the developing organism can be determined or is influenced by "something" in the gametes – should find expression.  No hypothesis about the nature of this "something" should be postulated or supported by it.  For that reason it seems simplest to use in isolation the last syllable <i>gen</i> from Darwin's well-known word, which alone is of interest to us, in order to replace, with it, the poor, ambiguous word <i>Anlage</i>.  Thus we will say simply "gene" and "genes" for "pangene" and "pangenes".  The word gene is completely free of any hypothesis ; it expresses only the established fact that in any case many traits of the organism are determined by specific, separable, and thus independent "conditions", "foundations", "plans" – in short, precisely what we want to call genes.)</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" <li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFHenig2000">Henig RM (2000). <span class="cs1-lock-registration" title="Free registration required"><a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/monkingardenlost00heni" rel="nofollow"><i>The Monk in the Garden: The Lost and Found Genius of Gregor Mendel, the Father of Genetics</i></a></span>. <li id="cite_note-de_Vries_1889-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-de_Vries_1889_31-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-de_Vries_1889_31-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1 cs1-prop-foreign-lang-source" id="CITEREFde_Vries1889"><a href="/wiki/Hugo_de_Vries" title="Hugo de Vries">de Vries H</a> (1889). <a class="external text" href="http://www.esp.org/books/devries/pangenesis/facsimile/" rel="nofollow"><i>Intracellulare Pangenese</i></a> [<i>Intracellular Pangenesis</i>] (in German). Translated by <a href="/wiki/C._Stuart_Gager" title="C. Stuart Gager">Gager CS</a>. Jena: Verlag von Gustav Fischer.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Intracellulare+Pangenese&amp;rft.place=Jena&amp;rft.pub=Verlag+von+Gustav+Fischer&amp;rft.date=1889&amp;rft.aulast=de+Vries&amp;rft.aufirst=H&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.esp.org%2Fbooks%2Fdevries%2Fpangenesis%2Ffacsimile%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGene"></span> Translated in 1908 from German to English by Open Court Publishing Co., Chicago, 1910</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bateson W. (1906) <a class="external text" href="https://www.biodiversitylibrary.org/item/206746#page/129/mode/1up" rel="nofollow">"The progress of genetic research"</a> <i>Report of the Third International Conference 1906 on Genetics</i>, W. Wilks, ed. London, England:  Royal Horticultural Society. pp. 90–97.  From p. 91:  " … the science itself [i.e. the study of the breeding and hybridisation of plants] is still nameless, and we can only describe our pursuit by cumbrous and often misleading periphrasis.  To meet this difficulty I suggest for the consideration of this Congress the term <i>Genetics</i>, which sufficiently indicates that our labors are devoted to the elucidation of the phenomena of heredity and variation:  in other words, to the physiology of Descent, with implied bearing on the theoretical problems of the evolutionist and the systematist, and application to the practical problems of breeders, whether of animals or plants."</span>
</li>
